<tr style="background:#f8eaba; border-top:2px solid transparent;">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Chemical_infobox#References" title="Wikipedia:Chemical infobox">InfoboxÂ references</a></td>

<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;"></td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;"></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Ethylenediaminetetraacetic acid</b> (<b>EDTA</b>), also known by <a href="#Names">several other names</a>, is a chemical used for both industrial and medical purposes.</p>
<p>It is an <a href="/wiki/Aminopolycarboxylic_acid" title="Aminopolycarboxylic acid">aminopolycarboxylic acid</a> and a colourless, water-soluble solid. Its <a href="/wiki/Conjugate_acid" title="Conjugate acid">conjugate base</a> is <b>ethylenediaminetetraacetate</b>. It is widely used to dissolve <a href="/wiki/Limescale" title="Limescale">limescale</a>. Its usefulness arises because of its role as a hexadentate ("six-toothed") <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chelating_agent" title="Chelating agent">chelating agent</a>, i.e., its ability to "sequester" <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">Ca<sup>2+</sup></a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Fe<sup>3+</sup></a>. After being bound by EDTA into a <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">metal complex</a>, metal ions remain in solution but exhibit diminished reactivity. EDTA is produced as several salts, notably disodium EDTA and calcium disodium EDTA.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In industry, EDTA is mainly used to sequester metal ions in aqueous solution. In the <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textile industry</a>, it prevents metal ion impurities from modifying colors of dyed products. In the <a href="/wiki/Pulp_and_paper_industry" title="Pulp and paper industry">pulp and paper industry</a>, EDTA inhibits the ability of metal ions, especially Mn<sup>2+</sup>, from catalyzing the <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>, which is used in "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totally_chlorine_free" title="Totally chlorine free">chlorine-free bleaching</a>". In a similar manner, EDTA is added to some food as a <a href="/wiki/Preservative" title="Preservative">preservative</a> or stabilizer to prevent catalytic oxidative decoloration, which is catalyzed by metal ions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-furia1964_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-furia1964-3">[3]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drinks</a> containing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascorbic_acid" title="Ascorbic acid">ascorbic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_benzoate" title="Sodium benzoate">sodium benzoate</a>, EDTA mitigates formation of <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogen</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The reduction of water hardness in laundry applications and the dissolution of scale in boilers both rely on EDTA and related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" title="Complex (chemistry)">complexants</a> to bind Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, as well as other metal ions. Once bound to EDTA, these metal centers tend not to form precipitates or to interfere with the action of the <a href="/wiki/Soap" title="Soap">soaps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Detergent" title="Detergent">detergents</a>. For similar reasons, cleaning solutions often contain EDTA.</p>
<p>The solubilization of ferric ions, at or below near neutral pH can be accomplished using EDTA. This property is useful in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> including hydroponics. However, given the pH dependence of ligand formation, EDTA is not helpful for improving Fe solubility in above neutral soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Otherwise, at near-neutral pH and above, iron(III) forms insoluble salts, which are less bioavailable to susceptible plant species. Aqueous [Fe(edta)]<sup>â</sup> is used for removing ("scrubbing") <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> from gas streams. This conversion is achieved by oxidizing the hydrogen sulfide to elemental sulfur, which is non-volatile:</p>
<dl>
<dd>2 [Fe(edta)]<sup>â</sup> + H<sub>2</sub>S â 2 [Fe(edta)]<sup>2â</sup> + <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">S</a> + 2 H<sup>+</sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>In this application, the ferric center is reduced to its ferrous derivative, which can then be reoxidized by air. In similar manner, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxides</a> are removed from gas streams using [Fe(edta)]<sup>2â</sup>. The oxidizing properties of [Fe(edta)]<sup>â</sup> are also exploited in photography, where it is used to solubilize silver particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>EDTA was used in the separation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lanthanide_metal" title="Lanthanide metal">lanthanide metals</a> by ion-exchange chromatography. Perfected by F.H. Spedding et al. in 1954, the method relies on the steady increase in stability constant of the lanthanide EDTA complexes with atomic number. Using sulfonated polystyrene beads and copper(II) as a retaining ion, EDTA causes the lanthanides to migrate down the column of resin while separating into bands of pure lanthanide. The lanthanides elute in order of decreasing atomic number. Due to the expense of this method, relative to counter-current solvent extraction, ion-exchange is now used only to obtain the highest purities of lanthanide (typically greater than 4N, 99.99%).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sodium_calcium_edetate" title="Sodium calcium edetate">Sodium calcium edetate</a></div>
<p>A specific salt of EDTA, known as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_calcium_edetate" title="Sodium calcium edetate">sodium calcium edetate</a>, is used to bind metal ions in the practice of <a href="/wiki/Chelation_therapy" title="Chelation therapy">chelation therapy</a>, e.g., for treating <a href="/wiki/Mercury_poisoning" title="Mercury poisoning">mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">lead poisoning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-r1-7">[7]</a></sup> It is used in a similar manner to remove excess iron from the body. This therapy is used to treat the complication of repeated blood transfusions, as would be applied to treat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thalassaemia" title="Thalassaemia">thalassaemia</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dentist" title="Dentist">Dentists</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endodontist" title="Endodontist">endodontists</a> use EDTA solutions to remove inorganic debris (<a href="/wiki/Smear_layer" title="Smear layer">smear layer</a>) and lubricate the canals in endodontics. This procedure helps prepare <a href="/wiki/Root_canal" title="Root canal">root canals</a> for obturation. Furthermore, EDTA solutions with the addition of a surfactant loosen up calcifications inside a root canal and allow instrumentation (canals shaping) and facilitate apical advancement of a file in a tight/calcified root canal towards the apex.</p>
<p>It serves as a preservative (usually to enhance the action of another preservative such as <a href="/wiki/Benzalkonium_chloride" title="Benzalkonium chloride">benzalkonium chloride</a> or <a href="/wiki/Thiomersal" title="Thiomersal">thiomersal</a>) in ocular preparations and eyedrops.</p>
<p>In evaluating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidney_function" title="Kidney function">kidney function</a>, the complex [Cr(edta)]<sup>â</sup> is administered intravenously and its filtration into the urine is monitored. This method is useful for evaluating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glomerular_filtration_rate" title="Glomerular filtration rate">glomerular filtration rate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>EDTA is used extensively in the analysis of blood. It is an <a href="/wiki/Anticoagulant" title="Anticoagulant">anticoagulant</a> for blood samples for <a href="/wiki/Complete_blood_count" title="Complete blood count">CBC/FBEs</a>.</p>
<p>EDTA is a slime dispersant, and has been found to be highly effective in reducing bacterial growth during implantation of <a href="/wiki/Intraocular_lens" title="Intraocular lens">intraocular lenses</a> (IOLs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_medicine">Alternative medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Alternative medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some alternative practitioners believe EDTA acts as an antioxidant, preventing free radicals from injuring blood vessel walls, therefore reducing <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-r1-7">[7]</a></sup> These ideas are as yet unsupported by scientific studies, and seem to contradict some currently accepted principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">U.S. FDA</a> has not approved it for the treatment of atherosclerosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmetics">Cosmetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cosmetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In shampoos, cleaners, and other personal care products, EDTA salts are used as a sequestering agent to improve their stability in air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanigan2002_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-lanigan2002-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_applications">Laboratory applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Laboratory applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the laboratory, EDTA is widely used for scavenging metal ions: In <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, ion depletion is commonly used to deactivate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metalloenzyme" title="Metalloenzyme">metal-dependent enzymes</a>, either as an assay for their reactivity or to suppress damage to DNA or proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In analytical chemistry, EDTA is used in <a href="/wiki/Complexometric_titration" title="Complexometric titration">complexometric titrations</a> and analysis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_hardness" title="Water hardness">water hardness</a> or as a <a href="/wiki/Masking_agent" title="Masking agent">masking agent</a> to sequester metal ions that would interfere with the analyses. EDTA finds many specialized uses in the biomedical laboratories, such as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veterinary" title="Veterinary">veterinary</a> <a href="/wiki/Ophthalmology" title="Ophthalmology">ophthalmology</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Collagenase" title="Collagenase">anticollagenase</a> to prevent the worsening of <a href="/wiki/Corneal_ulcers_in_animals" title="Corneal ulcers in animals">corneal ulcers in animals</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Tissue_culture" title="Tissue culture">tissue culture</a> EDTA is used as a chelating agent that binds to calcium and prevents joining of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadherins" title="Cadherins">cadherins</a> between cells, preventing clumping of cells grown in liquid suspension, or detaching adherent cells for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passaging" title="Passaging">passaging</a>. In histopathology, EDTA can be used as a decalcifying agent making it possible to cut sections using a microtome once the tissue sample is demineralised. EDTA is also known to inhibit a range of <a href="/wiki/Metalloproteinase" title="Metalloproteinase">metallopeptidases</a>, the method of inhibition occurs via the <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelation</a> of the metal ion required for catalytic activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> EDTA can also be used to test for bioavailability of heavy metals in sediments. However, EDTA may <i>influence</i> the bioavailability of metals in solution, which may pose concerns regarding its effects in the environment, especially given its widespread uses and applications.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Side_effects">Side effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Side effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>EDTA exhibits low acute toxicity with <a href="/wiki/Median_lethal_dose" title="Median lethal dose">LD<font size="-1"><sub>50</sub></font></a> (rat) of 2.0 g/kg to 2.2 g/kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-6">[6]</a></sup> It has been found to be both <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxicity" title="Cytotoxicity">cytotoxic</a> and weakly <a href="/wiki/Genotoxicity" title="Genotoxicity">genotoxic</a> in laboratory animals. Oral exposures have been noted to cause reproductive and developmental effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanigan2002_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-lanigan2002-12">[12]</a></sup> The same study by Lanigan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanigan2002_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-lanigan2002-12">[12]</a></sup> also found that both dermal exposure to EDTA in most cosmetic formulations and inhalation exposure to EDTA in aerosolized cosmetic formulations would produce exposure levels below those seen to be toxic in oral dosing studies.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis">Synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The compound was first described in 1935 by Ferdinand MÃ¼nz, who prepared the compound from <a href="/wiki/Ethylenediamine" title="Ethylenediamine">ethylenediamine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chloroacetic_acid" title="Chloroacetic acid">chloroacetic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Today, EDTA is mainly synthesised from <a href="/wiki/Ethylenediamine" title="Ethylenediamine">ethylenediamine</a> (1,2-diaminoethane), <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_cyanide" title="Sodium cyanide">sodium cyanide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This route yields the sodium salt, which can be converted in a subsequent step into the acid forms:</p>
<dl>
<dd>H<sub>2</sub>NCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>NH<sub>2</sub> + 4 CH<sub>2</sub>O + 4 NaCN + 4 H<sub>2</sub>O â (NaO<sub>2</sub>CCH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>NCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>N(CH<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>2</sub>Na)<sub>2</sub> + 4 NH<sub>3</sub></dd>
<dd>(NaO<sub>2</sub>CCH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>NCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>N(CH<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>2</sub>Na)<sub>2</sub> + 4 HCl â (HO<sub>2</sub>CCH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>NCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>N(CH<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>2</sub>H)<sub>2</sub> + 4 NaCl</dd>
</dl>
<p>This process is used to produce about 80 thousand tonnes each year. Impurities cogenerated by this route include <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrilotriacetic_acid" title="Nitrilotriacetic acid">nitrilotriacetic acid</a>; they arise from reactions of the ammonia coproduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>To describe EDTA and its various <a href="/wiki/Protonation" title="Protonation">protonated forms</a>, chemists distinguish between EDTA<sup>4â</sup>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjugate_base" title="Conjugate base">conjugate base</a> that is the <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a>, and H<sub>4</sub>EDTA, the <a href="/wiki/Precursor_(chemistry)" title="Precursor (chemistry)">precursor</a> to that ligand. At very low pH (very acidic conditions) the fully protonated H<sub>6</sub>EDTA<sup>2+</sup> form predominates, whereas at very high pH or very basic condition, the fully deprotonated EDTA<sup>4â</sup> form is prevalent. In this article, the term EDTA is used to mean H<sub>4â<i>x</i></sub>EDTA<sup><i>x</i>â</sup>, whereas in its complexes EDTA<sup>4â</sup> stands for the tetra-deprotonated ligand.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_chemistry_principles">Coordination chemistry principles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Coordination chemistry principles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metal-EDTA.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="744" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Metal-EDTA.svg/150px-Metal-EDTA.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Metal-EDTA.svg/225px-Metal-EDTA.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Metal-EDTA.svg/300px-Metal-EDTA.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metal-EDTA.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
MetalâEDTA <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chelate" title="Chelate">chelate</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination chemistry</a>, EDTA<sup>4â</sup> is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Aminopolycarboxylic_acid" title="Aminopolycarboxylic acid">aminopolycarboxylic acid</a> family of ligands. EDTA<sup>4â</sup> usually binds to a metal cation through its two amines and four carboxylates. Many of the resulting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" title="Complex (chemistry)">coordination compounds</a> adopt <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octahedral_geometry" title="Octahedral geometry">octahedral geometry</a>. Although of little consequence for its applications, these octahedral complexes are <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">chiral</a>. The anion [Co(EDTA)]<sup>â</sup> has been resolved into <a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">enantiomers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Many complexes of EDTA<sup>4â</sup> adopt more complex structures due to either the formation of an additional bond to water, i.e. seven-coordinate complexes, or the displacement of one carboxylate arm by water. The Fe(III) complex of EDTA is seven-coordinate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Early work on the development of EDTA was undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Gerold_Schwarzenbach" title="Gerold Schwarzenbach">Gerold Schwarzenbach</a> in the 1940s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> EDTA forms especially strong complexes with Mn(II), Cu(II), Fe(III), Pb(II) and Co(III).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several features of EDTA's complexes are relevant to its applications. First, because of its high <a href="/wiki/Denticity" title="Denticity">denticity</a>, this ligand has a high affinity for metal cations:</p>
<dl>
<dd>[Fe(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3+</sup> + H<sub>4</sub>EDTA â [Fe(EDTA)]<sup>â</sup> + 6 H<sub>2</sub>O + 4 H<sup>+</sup> (<a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_constant" title="Equilibrium constant"><i>K</i><sub>eq</sub></a> = 10<sup>25.1</sup>)</dd>
</dl>
<p>Written in this way, the <a href="/wiki/Stability_constants_of_complexes" title="Stability constants of complexes">equilibrium quotient</a> shows that metal ions compete with protons for binding to EDTA. Because metal ions are extensively enveloped by EDTA, their <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalytic properties</a> are often suppressed. Finally, since complexes of EDTA<sup>4â</sup> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anionic</a>, they tend to be highly soluble in water. For this reason, EDTA is able to dissolve deposits of metal oxides and carbonates.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_fate">Environmental fate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Environmental fate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abiotic_degradation">Abiotic degradation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Abiotic degradation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EDTA is in such widespread use that questions have been raised whether it is a <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">persistent organic pollutant</a>. While EDTA serves many positive functions in different industrial, pharmaceutical and other avenues, the longevity of EDTA can pose serious issues in the environment. The degradation of EDTA is slow. It mainly occurs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abiotic" title="Abiotic">abiotically</a> in the presence of sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most important process for the elimination of EDTA from surface waters is direct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">photolysis</a> at wavelengths below 400Â nm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Depending on the light conditions, the photolysis <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a> of Fe(III) EDTA in surface waters can range as low as 11.3 minutes up to more than 100 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Degradation of FeEDTA, but not EDTA itself, produces Fe complexes of ED3A, EDDA, and EDMA â 92% of EDDA and EDMA biodegrades in 20 hours while ED3A displays significantly higher resistance. Many environmentally abundant EDTA species (e.g., Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>) are more persistent.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodegradation">Biodegradation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Biodegradation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In many industrial wastewater treatment plants, EDTA elimination can be achieved at about 80% using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microorganisms" title="Microorganisms">microorganisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Resulting byproducts are ED3A and IDA â suggesting that both the backbone and acetyl groups were attacked. Some microorganisms have even been discovered to form nitrates out of EDTA but degrade optimally at moderately alkaline conditions of pH 9.0â9.5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several bacterial strains isolated from sewage treatment plants efficiently degrade EDTA. Specific strains include <a href="/wiki/Agrobacterium" title="Agrobacterium">Agrobacterium</a> radiobacter ATCC 55002<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and the sub-branches of <a href="/wiki/Proteobacteria" title="Proteobacteria">Proteobacteria</a> like BNC1, BNC2 <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nortemannl_1992_671.E2.80.93676_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nortemannl_1992_671.E2.80.93676-27">[27]</a></sup> and strain DSM 9103.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The three strains share similar properties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerobic_respiration" title="Aerobic respiration">aerobic respiration</a> and are classified as <a href="/wiki/Gram-negative_bacteria" title="Gram-negative bacteria">gram-negative bacteria</a>. Unlike photolysis, the chelated species is not exclusive to Fe(III) in order to be degraded. Rather, each strain uniquely consumes varying metal-EDTA complexes through several enzymatic pathways. Agrobacterium radiobacter only degrades Fe(III) EDTA <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nortemannl_1992_671.E2.80.93676_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nortemannl_1992_671.E2.80.93676-27">[27]</a></sup> while BNC1 and DSM 9103 are not capable of degrading Fe(III) EDTA and are more suited for CaEDTA, BaEDTA, MgEDTA and MnEDTA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> EDTA complexes require dissociation before degradation .</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives">Alternatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Alternatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Interest in environmental safety has brought up concerns about biodegradability in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aminopolycarboxylates" title="Aminopolycarboxylates">aminopolycarboxylates</a> such as EDTA. For example, under the 28-day <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">EN ISO</a> 7827 test Austrian paper and pulp industries must use chelating agents that have biodegradation levels over 70% or 80% (after 28 days).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intech_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-intech-30">[30]</a></sup> An increased interest in safety has led to the development and research of alternative chelating ligands which can still bind strongly to metal ions but also have a higher biodegradability and a lower content of nitrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intech_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-intech-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iminodisuccinic_acid_.28IDS.29">Iminodisuccinic acid (IDS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Iminodisuccinic acid (IDS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Commercially used since 1998, iminodisuccinic (IDS) acid biodegrades about 80% after only 7 days. IDS binds to calcium exceptionally well and forms stable compounds with other heavy metal ions. In addition to having a lower toxicity after chelation, the production of IDS is environment-friendly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intech_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-intech-30">[30]</a></sup> Specifically, IDS is degraded through the use of IDS-epimerase and CâN <a href="/wiki/Lyase" title="Lyase">lyase</a> found in <a href="/wiki/Agrobacterium_tumefaciens" title="Agrobacterium tumefaciens">Agrobacterium tumefaciens</a> (BY6) which can be harvested on a large scale. Additionally, the reactions catalyzed by both enzymes do not require any <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">cofactors</a> and can thus be applied directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polyaspartic_acid">Polyaspartic acid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Polyaspartic acid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polyaspartic_acid" title="Polyaspartic acid">Polyaspartic acid</a>, like IDS binds to calcium and other heavy metal ions. It has a higher value of 7.2 meq/g than does EDTA, which only has 6.0 meq/g.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intech_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-intech-30">[30]</a></sup> While it has a higher theoretical capacity, in practical applications it exhibits low efficiency in lower ion concentration solutions. It has many practical applications including corrosion inhibitors, waste water additives, and agricultural polymers. A Polyaspartic acid-based laundry detergent was the first laundry detergent in the world to receive the EU flower ecolabel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intech_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-intech-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethylenediamine-N.2CN.E2.80.B2-disuccinic_acid_.28EDDS.29">Ethylenediamine-<i>N</i>,<i>N</i>â²-disuccinic acid (EDDS)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ethylenediamine-N,Nâ²-disuccinic acid (EDDS)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a structural isomer of EDTA, <a href="/wiki/EDDS" title="EDDS">ethylenediamine-<i>N</i>,<i>N</i>â²-disuccinic acid</a> can exist as three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isomers" title="Isomers">isomers</a>: (<i>S</i>,<i>S</i>), (<i>R</i>,<i>S</i>)/(<i>S</i>,<i>R</i>) and (<i>R</i>,<i>R</i>), but only the <i>S</i>,<i>S</i>-isomer is readily biodegradable. EDDS exhibits a surprisingly high rate biodegradation at 83% in 20 days. Biodegradation rates also varies the different metal ions chelated. For example, the complexes of lead and zinc with EDDS have relatively the same stability but the lead complex is biodegrades more efficiently than the zinc complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intech_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-intech-30">[30]</a></sup> As of 2002, EDDS has been commercially prominent in Europe on a large scale with an estimated demand rate increase of about 15% each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intech_30-6"><a href="#cite_note-intech-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methylglycinediacetic_acid">Methylglycinediacetic acid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Methylglycinediacetic acid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Methylglycinediacetic acid (MGDA) is produced from <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intech_30-7"><a href="#cite_note-intech-30">[30]</a></sup> MGDA has a high rate of biodegradation &gt;68%, but unlike many other chelating agents can degrade without the assistance of adapted bacteria. Additionally, unlike EDDS or IDS, MGDA can withstand higher temperatures while maintaining a high stability as well as the entire pH range. As a result, the chelating strength of MGDA is stronger than many commercial chelating agents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intech_30-8"><a href="#cite_note-intech-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="L-glutamic_acid_N.2CN-diacetic_acid.2C_tetrasodium_salt_.28GLDA.29"><small>L</small>-glutamic acid <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-diacetic acid, tetrasodium salt (GLDA)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: L-glutamic acid N,N-diacetic acid, tetrasodium salt (GLDA)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aminopolycarboxylate-based chelates control metal ions in water-based systems.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_of_detection_and_analysis">Methods of detection and analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethylenediaminetetraacetic_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Methods of detection and analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The most sensitive method of detecting and measuring EDTA in biological samples is selected-reaction-monitoring <a href="/wiki/Capillary_electrophoresis" title="Capillary electrophoresis">capillary-electrophoresis</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_chromatography-mass_spectrometry" title="Liquid chromatography-mass spectrometry">mass-spectrometry</a> (abbreviation SRM-CE/MS), which has a <a href="/wiki/Detection_limit" title="Detection limit">detection limit</a> of 7.3Â ng/mL in human plasma and a <a href="/wiki/Detection_limit" title="Detection limit">quantitation limit</a> of 15Â ng/mL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheppard_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-sheppard-32">[32]</a></sup> This method works with sample volumes as small as ~7â8 nL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheppard_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-sheppard-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>EDTA has also been measured in non-alcoholic beverages using <a href="/wiki/High-performance_liquid_chromatography" title="High-performance liquid chromatography">high performance liquid chromatography</a> (HPLC) at a level of 2.0 Î¼g/mL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
